  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="review_1871217577" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1871217577" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1871217577?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060974480" href="/book/show/703103.Immortality">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=czech-republic-poland">czech-republic-poland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=postmodern-fiction">postmodern-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=paris">paris</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1871217577" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9761667489247599100">To become engaged within the first few pages of a book is always a good sign. However, at the back of my mind, history kept telling me that many other novels have started out in the stratosphere only to plummet to the bottom of the ocean. Milan Kundera's 'Immortality', starts great, gets better, and ended with a lump in my throat, and a soul that was struck by a chord. No actually, forget the single chord, this was more like an Orchestra going in full swing. Kundera has worked wonders here.<br/><br/>Witne</span>
  <span id="freeText9761667489247599100" style="display:none">To become engaged within the first few pages of a book is always a good sign. However, at the back of my mind, history kept telling me that many other novels have started out in the stratosphere only to plummet to the bottom of the ocean. Milan Kundera's 'Immortality', starts great, gets better, and ended with a lump in my throat, and a soul that was struck by a chord. No actually, forget the single chord, this was more like an Orchestra going in full swing. Kundera has worked wonders here.<br /><br />Witnessing a playful, sexual, yet sweet physical gesture of a woman by the swimming pool, begins. Kundera weaves a story around this starting gesture. Slowly introducing other characters that are part of her life and compares her life in the 20th century with another one a century earlier. It is an interesting perspective on what immortality is. What do people remember you for? is it what you have achieved?, or maybe just solely based on the perception that others have of you? Are you remembered only by your loved ones, or are you revered or scorned by the entire world? These are questions that will definitely get you thinking, and thinking plays an integral part in Immortality, as the words on each and every page only go so far, as Kundera puts the emphasis on the reader to sit comfy, and give his or hers full attention. (Tried reading on the metro, forget it), this is a book that pays off reading in seclusion as much as possible. In a skillful way, new characters silently crawl out of the woodwork, leaving you hanging, only to be bought up randomly somewhere else in some other context. This does keep you engrossed, it does also become a pain, but a pain worth putting with.<br /><br />Empathy is slowly drawn into the picture, as characters are slowly woven in an intermittently way, <br />that it strangely sexual and often quite perplexing. Kundera's characters acquire psychologies and histories, but they start out and continue to function chiefly as images, provocations: a man staring at a wall, or repeating a phrase; a woman arguing, putting on her glasses, shaking her head; a girl sitting in the middle of a major road amidst rushing traffic. These images are not illustrations of pre-formed thoughts, but they are not simply pieces of novelistic behaviour either. They are meetings between persons and notions, or more precisely, written, re-created, invented records of such meetings. He is clearly a most confident writer, I mean, Goethe &amp; Hemingway in conversation?, Genius!.<br /><br />There are some beautifully written passages of writing, likened to a philosophical voyage into Paradise, and there IS a wonderfully elegant and provocative story lurking under, indicating a second read may help untangle the knots of uncertainty, as Kundera teases the reader with provocations and paradoxes that require some deep pondering. This is a book without conclusion, there really isn't a beginning, middle or end. <br /><br />After the closing pages, I was left moved, awestruck, and slightly mentally exhausted.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9761667489247599100" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_858355894" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/858355894" />
    <a title="Violet wells" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells"><img alt="Violet wells" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442260743p2/4196681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/858355894?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Violet wells" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Violet wells" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells">Violet wells</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=faves">faves</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer858355894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1192231900493796759">On one level you could reduce this novel to the sour grapes of a man who’s getting old and losing his privileged place in the world. Not that this belittles its aspiration or wisdom because how the self changes with age, how the declining façade impacts the core, is a fascinating and rich subject. Kundera suggests the self doesn’t significantly change from within but rather is bullied out of its natural gait by the way people see us, by the images they impose on us. Even we ourselves are constra</span>
  <span id="freeText1192231900493796759" style="display:none">On one level you could reduce this novel to the sour grapes of a man who’s getting old and losing his privileged place in the world. Not that this belittles its aspiration or wisdom because how the self changes with age, how the declining façade impacts the core, is a fascinating and rich subject. Kundera suggests the self doesn’t significantly change from within but rather is bullied out of its natural gait by the way people see us, by the images they impose on us. Even we ourselves are constrained to represent our lives with isolated images because memory, he tells us, is incapable of retaining anything but snapshots of time, isolated frames which no effort of will can restore to a detailed and continuous home movie. We are confined to the snapshots memory selects to preserve. And ultimately, in death, we become how people remember us. We become a series of snapshots, an image. <br /><br />At the time of writing this novel Kundera was pretty much guaranteed immortality. He’s earned his place among the immortals of literature. Understandable then that he should ponder what form this immortality will take. In one episode he has Goethe and Hemingway discuss their posthumous lives. Hemingway is unhappy that his books have become eclipsed by the innumerable biographies of his life. There’s also a fabulous section about Goethe’s relationship with a young girl called Bettina. To Goethe Bettina appears nothing but an episode. Little does he know that this largely inconsequential girl will become one of the editors of his posthumous life. <br /><br />"No episode is a priori condemned to remain an episode forever, for every event, no matter how trivial, conceals within itself the possibility of sooner or later becoming the cause of other events and thus changing into a story or an adventure. Episodes are like land mines. The majority of them never explode, but the most unremarkable of them may someday turn into a story that will prove fateful to you." This quote is perhaps the underlying mantra of this novel. It’s a novel of philosophical episodes which playfully mocks the conventions of the novel. "Dramatic tension is the real curse of the novel, because it transforms everything, even the most beautiful pages, even the most surprising scenes and observations merely into steps leading to the final resolution, in which the meaning of everything that preceded is concentrated." Towards the end of Immortality Milan Kundera is sitting by the same swimming pool which opens the novel with two of his characters. He is surprised these two characters know each other. <br /><br />Perhaps one test of a masterpiece is that it should improve on a second reading. I really enjoyed this but I didn’t quite love it as much as I did when I first read it. Some elements seemed dated, like his obsessive whining about noise pollution. Guitars and motorbikes especially cited as enemies of civilised life. We now face much worse forms of pollution and his singling out of urban noise levels made him appear a grumpy old man at times. Also some of his views on sex were those of an ageing womaniser who still can’t help seeing women almost exclusively through his libido. As his starting point Kundera shows us an elderly woman performing an alluring gesture she had used as a young girl. He finds the gesture “charming” but for him it’s only the gesture that has charm and elegance “while the body and face no longer had any charm. It was the charm of a gesture drowning in the charmlessness of the body.” This of course is the viewpoint of a sexually predatory male. Elderly women for most of us are no less capable of performing charming gestures than anyone else. Some of the most beautiful and haunting gestures I have seen have been performed by elderly people. Ironically Kundera’s posthumous life might be influenced in part by these kind of observations of his. He too, like Hemingway, might be complaining to Goethe in an afterlife that he has been misrepresented by biographies.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1192231900493796759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3757313775392912985">One of 3 books I would take to a desert island. A beautiful discussion on the nature of our legacy, what we decide to leave or not leave when we die. How that changes in time and how we can not do anything about it. Especially relevant to artists and writers. Delves into the nature of love and families while he is at it. It would take a life time to discuss this book.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_161213936" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/161213936" />
    <a title="Jenn(ifer)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/780933-jenn-ifer"><img alt="Jenn(ifer)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579713607p2/780933.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/161213936?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenn(ifer)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenn(ifer)" href="/user/show/780933-jenn-ifer">Jenn(ifer)</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060932381" href="/book/show/397256.Immortality">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/780933-jenn-ifer?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer161213936" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8627307068755706275">I’m not going to give this book a “star” rating because can’t decide on one. There were parts of the novel that I found fascinating, parts that were completely confusing, and parts that were downright irrelevant. The beginning of the novel is very engaging. It sucks the reader right in. I was immediately interested in Kundera’s philosophy. Case in point: “There is a certain part of all of us that lives outside of time. Perhaps we become aware of our age only at exceptional moments and most of th</span>
  <span id="freeText8627307068755706275" style="display:none">I’m not going to give this book a “star” rating because can’t decide on one. There were parts of the novel that I found fascinating, parts that were completely confusing, and parts that were downright irrelevant. The beginning of the novel is very engaging. It sucks the reader right in. I was immediately interested in Kundera’s philosophy. Case in point: “There is a certain part of all of us that lives outside of time. Perhaps we become aware of our age only at exceptional moments and most of the time we are ageless.” I also loved when he proposed the idea about a married couple being asked at the time of death whether they want to spend eternity together or never see each other again. Why didn’t he expound upon this? I think he let too many good ideas drop off.<br /><br />Unfortunately, as Kudera begins fucking with the time-line, it gets really convoluted and spits the reader right back out. I get what he’s going for, problem is, it gets a little clumsy. The language is still beautiful, and there are some special moments: “I can savor drowsing and waking, that marvelous swinging between wakefulness and sleep which in itself is enough to keep us from regretting our birth.” <br /><br />I loved his observations about classical artists portrayal of expressions in paintings and sculptures. “A face is beautiful because it reveals the presence of thought whereas the moment of laughter man does not think.” <br /><br />In the end, I thought it started really strong, got bogged down in the middle and ended on a high note.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8627307068755706275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1369419287" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1369419287" />
    <a title="Ted" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7213075-ted"><img alt="Ted" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101210p2/7213075.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1369419287?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ted" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ted" href="/user/show/7213075-ted">Ted</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780802111111" href="/book/show/920138.Immortality">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone afraid of post-moderism</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=reviews-liked">reviews-liked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=postmodern-lit">postmodern-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1369419287" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7526021116837909229"><i>Playful ... moving ... didactic ... erotic ... misleading ... satisfying ... </i><br/><br/>A couple years ago I bought four of Kundera’s books at once – this one, The Joke, Ignorance, and of course The Unbearable Lightness of Being – never having read a word written by him. <br/><br/>Immortality is the first that I’ve read.<br/><br/>I don’t know much about postmodernism in literature. I’ve read some novels that are called postmodernist. But there are many more … <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[Here’s a list of ten that I found on line.<br/><br/>Sot Weed F</span></span>
  <span id="freeText7526021116837909229" style="display:none"><i>Playful ... moving ... didactic ... erotic ... misleading ... satisfying ... </i><br><br>A couple years ago I bought four of Kundera’s books at once – this one, The Joke, Ignorance, and of course The Unbearable Lightness of Being – never having read a word written by him. <br><br>Immortality is the first that I’ve read.<br><br>I don’t know much about postmodernism in literature. I’ve read some novels that are called postmodernist. But there are many more … <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Here’s a list of ten that I found on line.<br><br>Sot Weed Factor – I read decades ago, loved it<br>Blood and Guts in High School – never heard of it, tho looks interesting<br>Wittgenstein’s Mistress – looks very tempting<br>Pale Fire – on my Maybe list – I should read it<br>Gravity’s Rainbow – read decades ago, liked it – probably didn’t understand it tho<br>House of Leaves – it’s said to be good, but this is the sort of postmodern novel I can’t abide at my age – no, no, no<br>Labyrinths (Borges) – have it partially read, love it<br>Sixty Stories (Barthelme) – was off my radar, now it’s on it<br>Infinite Jest – have it, unread<br>The Recognitions – on my Maybe list – I think I’ll eventually get to it.<br><br>I’ll also mention Cloud Atlas, which I read and loved.<br>and Textermination, which I have but frankly is probably beyond my comprehension, and may never be read<br><br>I’m sure there are others I have which would qualify. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>Some readers rave about a certain type of postmodernist novel, one which present the reader with a dense thicket of cross-referencing passages, unreliable narrators, made up words – basically a thousand (or 10,000) piece jigsaw puzzle with no picture of what it’s supposed to look like. This is definitely not for me (I reiterate – if you read the spoiler). If I want to work hard, I’ll do it in non-fiction reading, not a damn novel. Fiction is mostly for pleasure at my age. <br><br>Immortality has faint traces of this sort of postmodernism. But not enough to deter me. I found it a very interesting read, <i>to say the least.</i><br><br>The first character we gradually discover is Agnes. Agnes is built from a gesture that the narrator sees an older woman make at a swimming pool. <blockquote>At that instant I felt a pang in my heart! That smile and that gesture belonged to a twenty-year-old girl! Her arm rose with bewitching ease. It was as if she were playfully tossing a brightly colored ball to her lover. That smile and that gesture had charm and elegance, while the face and the body no longer had any charm. It was the charm of a gesture drowning in the charmlessness of the body ... I was strangely moved. And then the word Agnes entered my mind. Agnes. I had never known a woman by that name.</blockquote>But wait. Is this elderly woman, charmless, <i>possibly named Agnes</i>, really a character in the novel? And what about that first-person narrator? Is he not a character, in a sense?<br><br>To answer the second first, no, the narrator is in fact Milan Kundera, the author. Not fictional, so far as I know. <br><br>The first question requires a couple more pages of reading. Then, <blockquote>When I wake up, at almost eight-thirty, I try to picture Agnes. She is lying, like me, in a wide bed. The right side of the bed is empty. Who could her husband be? Clearly, somebody who leaves the house early on Saturday mornings. That’s why she is alone, sweetly swinging between waking and sleeping.<br><br>Then she gets up. Facing her is a TV set, standing on one long, storklike leg. She throws her nightgown over the tube, like a white, tasseled theater curtain. She stands close to the bed, and for the first time I see her naked. Agnes, the heroine of my novel. I can’t take my eyes off this beautiful woman, and as if sensing my gaze she hurries off to the adjoining room to get dressed.<br><br>Who is Agnes? … Agnes sprang from the gesture of that sixty-year-old woman at the pool … whose features are already fading from my memory. At the time, that gesture aroused in me immense, inexplicable nostalgia, and this nostalgia gave birth to the woman I call Agnes.</blockquote><br><br>So we’re off. This first section of the novel, “The face”, introduces the people surrounding Agnes – her mother and father, her husband Paul, her daughter Brigitte, her sister Laura.<br><br>These are the remaining sections of the novel<br><br>Two. Immortality. An essay about Goethe, his longing for immortality, and a bizarre relationship he had (and/or tried not to have) with <i>Bettina</i> – aka Bettina von Arnim. Fascinating. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1455995450i/18178403._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Johann Wolfgang von Goethe in 1828</b> (Wiki)<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1455995450i/18178404._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Bettina von Arnim as drawn by Ludwig Emil Grimm during the first decade of the 19th century</b> (Wiki)<br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>Three. Fighting. The longest section of the novel, the story of the relationships between Agnes and the other characters, including newly introduced characters. The subparts have their own titles, such as The sisters; Dark glasses; Older woman, younger man; Imagology; A complete ass; To be a victim of one’s fame; Professor Avenarius; The gesture of longing for immortality.<br><br>Four. Homo sentimentalis. Back to Goethe, including (like other sections) many comments on, and <i>conversations with</i>, such as Rilke, Hemingway, Rimbaud, Aristotle, Stendahl, Schnitzler. <br><br>Five. Chance. Swinging back and forth between a conversation between the narrator and Prof. Avenarius, himself (wholly or in part) a character in the novel. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[An amusing passage.<blockquote>Avenarius lapsed into a perplexed silence. After a while he asked me, in a kindly voice, “And what will your novel be called?”<br>“<i>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</i>.”<br>“I think somebody has already written that.”<br>“I did! But I was wrong about the title then. That title was supposed to belong to the novel I’m writing right now.”<br>We stopped talking and concentrated on the taste of the wine and the duck.</blockquote> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>Six. The dial. A connected story about Rubens. Not the noted artist, but a contemporary man, once having had ambitions to <i>be</i> an artist, but instead consumed by a desire to explore the phases and ages of love. The connection to the rest of the novel is only slowly revealed. The “dial” variously described as the dial of a clock, then “Europe’s dial”, ultimately the <i>dial of life</i><br><br>Seven. The celebration. Short. A reunion. Professor Avenarius, the narrator, the other characters (as it were, in search of an author). The celebration …(perhaps a real <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<blockquote>I quickly went out into the street.<br><br>The roadway was filled with cars honking incessantly. Motorcycles drove up on the sidewalk and snaked their was between pedestrians. I thought of Agnes. It was precisely two years ago that I had first imagined her, while reclining in a deck chair upstairs in the health club, waiting for Avenarius. That was the reason why today I had ordered a bottle of wine. I had finished the novel and I wanted to celebrate it in the place where the first idea for it had been born.<br><br>The cars were honking their horns, and I heard the shouts of angry people. It was in such circumstances that Agnes longed to buy a forget-me-not, a single forget-me-not stem; she longed to hold it before her eyes as a last, scarcely visible trace of beauty.</blockquote> (and as) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> … a longing for immortality, the unbearable longing to not be forgotten.<br><br><br><br><br><i>Playful ... moving ... didactic ... erotic ... misleading ... satisfying ... </i><br><br>Did I like it? Guess.<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1455995450i/18178405._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Milan Kundera, in 1980</b> (wiki)<br></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="7526021116837909229" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1975971056" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1975971056" />
    <a title="Chris_P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p"><img alt="Chris_P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558554241p2/44309339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1975971056?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris_P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris_P" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p">Chris_P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44309339-chris-p?shelf=5-star-diamonds">5-star-diamonds</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1975971056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8593231673157330560">There's something unique about Kundera, apart from the obvious. He has so many elements that I otherwise dislike, like the fact that he intervenes directly or that he interrupts the plot every so often to endlessly talk about his views, and yet his books seem to have something that grabs me by the neck every time, making me devour 150 pages in a single day! Up until now, <i>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</i> was my favorite Kundera, with the others following not very close (not too far either). <i>Immo</i></span>
  <span id="freeText8593231673157330560" style="display:none">There's something unique about Kundera, apart from the obvious. He has so many elements that I otherwise dislike, like the fact that he intervenes directly or that he interrupts the plot every so often to endlessly talk about his views, and yet his books seem to have something that grabs me by the neck every time, making me devour 150 pages in a single day! Up until now, <i>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</i> was my favorite Kundera, with the others following not very close (not too far either). <i>Immortality</i> reaches the level of brilliance of the Lightness, which I'll have to read again if I am to decide which one's better.<br /><br />I know it's a cliche, but <i>Immortality</i> read me as much as I read it. It might well be writing a review on me right now! In fact, reading it was suspiciously close to making love. (If you, readers, find the idea of making love to a book absurd, do think again!) Where was I? Yes, <i>Immortality</i> reads the reader, making its points not only visible, but almost touchable. It's a two-way relationship that, in the end, leaves you enlightened and scarred, like every relationship that is worth your while does. <br /><br />Kundera once again builds his characters out of the most tangible materials, so much so that they seem ready to jump out of the paper and walk right in front of you. However, it's not just the characters that make this a masterpiece. The very subject of immortality is particularly delicate and hard to handle, let alone when it's brought down to our every-day lives. Kundera makes his points without hesitating to put huge personalities to the equation, like Goethe and Hemingway who, by the way, share a couple of geniously crafted scenes together. <br /><br />Genius. That's the word. <i>Immortality</i> isn't a book one can easily talk about. You can never adequately describe an intense experience, can you? I've said more than I thought I could, so let's just leave it at that. Five immortal stars.<br /><br />P.S.: It's a shame that it was never adapted by Buñuel or Antonioni or Kaufman even!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8593231673157330560" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6986126214446900041"><b>Cerebral Crosswinds over Parisian Fields</b><br/>4.5 stars<br/><br/><i>Immortality</i> is so rejuvenating to the reading experience, pulling Goethe and Hemingway from beyond, effortlessly using magical literary devices in pleasing ways.   <span id="freeText6986126214446900041" style="display:none"><b>Cerebral Crosswinds over Parisian Fields</b><br />4.5 stars<br /><br /><i>Immortality</i> is so rejuvenating to the reading experience, pulling Goethe and Hemingway from beyond, effortlessly using magical literary devices in pleasing ways. It should leave an indelible mark on the way you view a novel. One cannot describe the plot/theme without spoiling the trip.<br /><br />When you begin reading Kundera, if you haven't read him before, it may help you to understand that he involves the writer (often a fictional character in himself) in the reading process. That is to say, he often adopts the personna of a friendly writer, much like Roth's Zuckerman, and will allow a view into his thinking in the writing of the novel. Here, this novel begins with his (the writer's) imagining the life of a woman who fascinates him as he lounges beside a hotel pool.<br /><br />I found this the best place to begin the endeavor of reading Milan Kundera. Enjoy.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/2431689-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer244947138" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2742108243044654664">This was my first time reading Kundera. I picked it up in a bookstore on a whim and was completely enthralled. This author has a way of saying things I've always wanted to say, but never found the words to do so. He has a talent for observation that is cleverly, if not blatantly, philosophical. He's also very funny. <br/>But enough about him, since one of his main points is that we all concern ourselves way too much with the personal lives of creative artists rather than their actual work. Hence, I s</span>
  <span id="freeText2742108243044654664" style="display:none">This was my first time reading Kundera. I picked it up in a bookstore on a whim and was completely enthralled. This author has a way of saying things I've always wanted to say, but never found the words to do so. He has a talent for observation that is cleverly, if not blatantly, philosophical. He's also very funny. <br />But enough about him, since one of his main points is that we all concern ourselves way too much with the personal lives of creative artists rather than their actual work. Hence, I suppose, the reason he gives himself a role in the storyline of the book, Alfred-Hitchcock style. He also throws in Goethe, Beethoven, Napoleon, and Hemingway and several others to help explain his ideas, and coins terms like "ridiculous immortality" and "hypertrophy of the soul" to give us his own "sound bites" for our minds to chew on.<br />The main storyline reads kind of like a modern Greek tragedy with a twist of French existentialism and several intermissions. I don't know if I could recommend it to everyone, but for creative types, depressives, beatniks, and anyone else who has felt like they didn't fit in with the rest of the world, this is a great book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2742108243044654664" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2134273322?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mutasim Billah " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mutasim Billah " href="/user/show/20438238-mutasim-billah">Mutasim Billah </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780802111111" href="/book/show/920138.Immortality">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20438238-mutasim-billah?shelf=france">france</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2134273322" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8885347051301730580"><i>“To be mortal is the most basic human experience, and yet man has never been able to accept it, grasp it, and behave accordingly. Man doesn't know how to be mortal. And when he dies, he doesn't even know how to be dead.” </i><br/><br/>I was torn between loving and hating this book but eventually I've come to like it a lot more with time. Love, because there are few writers with the gift of mixing fable and allegory, facts and fiction and metaphors into a delicious recipe called "the novel" like Kundera has sh</span>
  <span id="freeText8885347051301730580" style="display:none"><i>“To be mortal is the most basic human experience, and yet man has never been able to accept it, grasp it, and behave accordingly. Man doesn't know how to be mortal. And when he dies, he doesn't even know how to be dead.” </i><br /><br />I was torn between loving and hating this book but eventually I've come to like it a lot more with time. Love, because there are few writers with the gift of mixing fable and allegory, facts and fiction and metaphors into a delicious recipe called "the novel" like Kundera has shown here. Hate, because this, in my eyes, is where his misogyny was most open.<br /><br />Like every other Kundera novel, the underlying theme of the writing lies in the title, meaning the book is about "immortality". This "immortality" isn't just mentioned in the physical concept of life and death but also in the form of fame and the impact a famous person leaves on humanity, their stubborn footprints left in the society in a war against time. The story has several characters, some history and thought-provoking fables, all describing the futile struggle of Man against the ephemeral quality of His image.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8885347051301730580" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1288145314" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1288145314" />
    <a title="Junta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38230974-junta"><img alt="Junta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1447099175p2/38230974.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1288145314?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Junta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Junta" href="/user/show/38230974-junta">Junta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Junta by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/38414329-susie">Susie</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=czech">czech</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=kundera">kundera</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1288145314" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7563945122864944989">Of my favourite authors, Haruki Murakami is like my enigmatic, bohemian tutor; Fyodor Dostoyevsky my reclusive, yet highly proficient counselor; and Milan Kundera an amiable uncle full of wit and wisdom. While Kundera's stories in themselves are beautiful, it's his philosophical insights and observations about mankind which I can't get enough of - yes, this is someone who I can truly respect in raising their voice about the human condition.<blockquote>When someone is young, he is not capable of conceiving o</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText7563945122864944989" style="display:none">Of my favourite authors, Haruki Murakami is like my enigmatic, bohemian tutor; Fyodor Dostoyevsky my reclusive, yet highly proficient counselor; and Milan Kundera an amiable uncle full of wit and wisdom. While Kundera's stories in themselves are beautiful, it's his philosophical insights and observations about mankind which I can't get enough of - yes, this is someone who I can truly respect in raising their voice about the human condition.<blockquote>When someone is young, he is not capable of conceiving of time as a circle, but thinks of it as a road leading forward to ever-new horizons; he does not yet sense that his life contains just a single theme; he will come to realise it only when his life begins to enact its first variations.</blockquote>There are countless themes swimming inside this novel (or are they merely variations on life and death?), and every few pages you're left wondering how you feel about what he's just touched on - have I ever thought about this?; yes, he has put it perfectly; that's what I've always wanted to put into words but never could!<blockquote>'Just imagine living in a world without mirrors. You'd dream about your face and imagine it as an outer reflection of what is inside you. And then, when you reached forty, someone would put a mirror before you for the first time in your life. Imagine your fright! You'd see the face of a stranger. And you'd know quite clearly what you are unable to grasp: your face is not you.'</blockquote>One of the strongest forces in our lives, and perhaps a natural human constant, is identifying with ourselves. Could you go a day without looking in the mirror? Isn't your name so dear to you that it even feels like it's something you yourself created out of talent and inspiration? Are our gestures, familial relations, mannerisms in making love and what we hold as our own peculiarities, actually unique when the sheer number of people on Earth suggests otherwise? If we created art, do we wish for its immortality or our own as the creator? Just how well do we think we know how others perceive us?<br /><br />Reading Kundera, you're put face-to-face with many questions about your present and identity, but you're also pulled back to your past, with your childhood, family home, regrets and first steps in love; as well as the future, where your dreams and aspirations lie, what we want in love and matrimony, how we want to be seen by the world in life, and in death. Personally I think too little of the present, sandwiched between my past and future.<blockquote>'I long for an experiment that would examine, by means of electrodes attached to the human head, exactly how much of one's life a person devotes to the present, how much to the memories and how much to the future. This would let us know who a man really is in relation to his time. What human time really is.'</blockquote>I especially liked Agnes in this novel; I felt that her own world and dignity were precious to her over anything else, and I could relate to that. While she realises she loved and truly connected with her father, there's always an insurmountable gap between her and her mother, sister, lovers, even husband and daughter.<blockquote>Agnes subtracts from her self everything that is exterior and borrowed, in order to come closer to her sheer essence (even with the risk that zero lurks at the bottom of the subtraction). Laura's method is precisely the opposite: in order to make her self ever more visible, perceivable, seizable, sizeable, she keeps adding to it more and more attributes and she attempts to identify herself with them (with the risk that the essence of the self may be buried by the additional attributes).</blockquote>The only reason I didn't give this book five stars is that it fell just short of the standards of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9717.The_Unbearable_Lightness_of_Being" title="The Unbearable Lightness of Being by Milan Kundera" rel="nofollow noopener">The Unbearable Lightness of Being</a> which touched me deeper, but I must say that <i>Immortality</i> easily overpowers most novels I've rated four stars. If you haven't read Kundera before, I highly recommend these two, in my eyes, works of art that speak through their wisdom, insights, and most of all, love.<br /><br />August 5, 2015<br /><br />2016 edit: Kundera isn't on my Favourite Authors list any more, but I still love this work and <i>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7563945122864944989" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_758747544" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/758747544" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/758747544?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer758747544" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6777342174913381466"><br/>This is a great book and I wish I had discovered it years ago, when it was translated from the Czech in 1991. I liked it much more than Unbearable Lightness of Being. A blurb says the book thoroughly explores “the great themes of existence” which is quite a task to pull off in 345 pages, but Kundera makes quite a dent in those themes. Where to start? There is a story: a couple has a daughter; the wife dies, and eventually the man marries his dead wife’s sister. That’s pretty much the plot. But a</span>
  <span id="freeText6777342174913381466" style="display:none"><br />This is a great book and I wish I had discovered it years ago, when it was translated from the Czech in 1991. I liked it much more than Unbearable Lightness of Being. A blurb says the book thoroughly explores “the great themes of existence” which is quite a task to pull off in 345 pages, but Kundera makes quite a dent in those themes. Where to start? There is a story: a couple has a daughter; the wife dies, and eventually the man marries his dead wife’s sister. That’s pretty much the plot. But as this story is told, the author compares the traits of the main characters and their attitudes toward life. For example, the first wife is serious, organized and not much fun. The younger sister is reckless, impulsive, at time suicidal, and has a series of affairs with men. The man is hard-working, reflective, and bit baffled by life. Kundera speculates on philosophical themes around this simple plot. He writes about gestures as memes; solitude; bodies and faces; sex, love and lust; chance and coincidence; cameras and privacy; journalism and public opinion pools, Watergate, and what he calls imagology (kind of like symbolism). He compares a highway that by-passes space with a road that allows you to experience the landscape. He invents a character, Professor Avenarius, who is so upset by the dominance of the automobile in cities that he randomly slashes tires as he jogs. <br /><br />And, of course, given the title of the book and Kundera’s prominence as an author, he speculates on legacies and how you can’t control how history will view you. At times the book has the flavor of a meta-novel as in some chapters the author talks directly to the reader and tells us about the process of writing and even refers to characters in his earlier works. In other chapters he holds imaginary conversations with folks like Goethe and Hemingway and spends a bit of time on the odd life-long relationship between Goethe and a young woman, Bettina, who may have been the world’s first self-publicist way back in the early 1800’s. He points out that until recently people knew everything through their own experience: how bread is baked; how a house is built; where meat comes from, etc. He writes about categorizing people by how much time they spend thinking of the past, present or future. Indeed, the great themes of existence, reflected upon by a great thinker. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6777342174913381466" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText18430496428070114558" style="display:none">If you could live forever with your spouse in heaven after you pass, would you? "How do you live in a world with which you disagree? How to live with people when you share neither their suffering nor their joys? When you know that you don't belong among them?"<br /><br />Agnes and Laura, sisters with opposite traits ground Kundera's novel of variations as he weaves fantasized personalities of the past like Goethe and Hemingway along with himself and friend as narrator to create opportunities to think about who we are - a person who seeks immortality by leaving some kind of mark on this world - or a person who wants to leave without leaving a trace behind.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14453017378448833548"><br/>I don't know why I love this book, I hated it so much. Completely broke my heart, first book ever to do so, it was just so real. It was real enough to be fictitious, reality too harshly dealt with here would be my biggest woe. First book to make me fall in love with death, and I have already read Fools Die by that time. Ever the realist, both of you girls, you want real? What's more real than this book even when it's not. Characters utterly refusing to stay fictional. Oddly enough, I read this d</span>
  <span id="freeText14453017378448833548" style="display:none"><br />I don't know why I love this book, I hated it so much. Completely broke my heart, first book ever to do so, it was just so real. It was real enough to be fictitious, reality too harshly dealt with here would be my biggest woe. First book to make me fall in love with death, and I have already read Fools Die by that time. Ever the realist, both of you girls, you want real? What's more real than this book even when it's not. Characters utterly refusing to stay fictional. Oddly enough, I read this during the time I was winning all of my battles in my mind. <br /><br />Immortality was the first time I dabbled into speculative fiction, finding out I rather like the surreal genre. Maybe that wasn't such a good thing, this had a profound impact on me and influenced me greatly. It turns out I am highly susceptible to the strange, figures huh? But it was a taste. My first, ineffable and indelible; as first forays usually are. A drink before the War. Now I want more. Gimme more, Warpaint. What I liked most about it is that Kundera made his characters into our reflection and made sure we didn't like what we see.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer514361473" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11263655560280034294">2019-12-03:<br/>This is my third time through this, perhaps Kundera's last "great" novel (in the sense of expansiveness which is lacking in his later novellas, as in Philip Roth's own post-"American Trilogy" work), and, as always, I am not sure I completely got it (or to be completely honest, I am <i>completely</i> sure that I did <i>not</i> quite get it), which is why I will keep coming back to him, circling around his mysterious imagination like the nocturnal moth around the bare lightbulb that closes <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9717.The_Unbearable_Lightness_of_Being' title='The Unbearable Lightness of Being by Milan Kundera' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Unbear</a></span>
  <span id="freeText11263655560280034294" style="display:none">2019-12-03:<br />This is my third time through this, perhaps Kundera's last "great" novel (in the sense of expansiveness which is lacking in his later novellas, as in Philip Roth's own post-"American Trilogy" work), and, as always, I am not sure I completely got it (or to be completely honest, I am <i>completely</i> sure that I did <i>not</i> quite get it), which is why I will keep coming back to him, circling around his mysterious imagination like the nocturnal moth around the bare lightbulb that closes <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9717.The_Unbearable_Lightness_of_Being" title="The Unbearable Lightness of Being by Milan Kundera" rel="nofollow noopener">The Unbearable Lightness of Being</a>. And hey, this also happens to be my hundredth review here on GR, so I will allow the author himself to pour me some of that tasty wine he and several of his characters partake of in the book's final pages as they meet to celebrate the approaching end of this very novel....<br /><br />I am fascinated by his repeated celebration of and undermining of the centrality of the European novel (specifically that trajectory of it which takes in Cervantes, Sterne, Flaubert, Musil and Kafka) as the previous century draws to its close--what his postmodernist character Paul cynically says about trajectory of European fine art could easily be applied to the novel or to the trajectory of the modern arts plural/in general: <blockquote> <i>[...] his ideas were getting more and more exaggerated and provocative. He said, "High culture is nothing but a child of that European perversion called history, the obsession we have with going forward, with considering the sequence of generations a relay race in which everyone surpasses his predecessor, only to be surpassed by his successor. Without this relay race called history there would be no European art and what characterizes it: a longing for originality, a longing for change. Robespierre, Napoleon, Beethoven, Stalin, Picasso, they're all runners in the relay race, they all belong in the same stadium." <br />"Beethoven and Stalin belong together?" asked the Bear with icy irony. <br />"Of course, no matter how much that may shock you. War and culture, those are the two poles of Europe, her heaven and hell, her glory and shame, and they cannot be separated from each other. When one comes to an end, the other will end also, and one cannot end without the other. The fact that no war has broken out in Europe for fifty years is connected in some mysterious way with the fact that for fifty years no new Picasso has appeared either."</i>(111)</blockquote>Of course, that would mean that Kundera lumps himself into that fifty years of postwar <i>Nada</i>, and while it is always dangerous to seek the author's voice in that of his characters, in <i> The ULB</i> he intrudes upon the narrative to inform us that each of his characters crosses a border or boundary of some sort which he himself has remained behind, so it is safe to say that the ongoing (ir)relevance of art in post-modern times to the wider "culture" as a whole is something that continues to fascinate, nay, <i>haunt</i> him --which at times tempts me to see his novels as explicit psychogeographies if not <i>psychomachias</i> of MK himself.<br /><br />Further along these lines, what Paul says much later in the book about Mahler suggests a late-20C weariness with the very notion of "Great" art (even as his narrator's ongoing fascination with Goethe continually suggests otherwise): <blockquote><i>Everything is worked through, thought through, felt through, nothing has been left to chance, but that enormous perfection overwhelms us, it surpasses the capacity of our memory, our ability to concentrate, so that even the most fanatically attentive listener will grasp no more than one-hundredth of the symphony, and certainly it will be this one-hundredth that Mahler cared about the least."<br />His idea, so obviously correct, cheered him up, whereas I was becoming sadder and sadder: if a reader skips a single sentence of my novel he won't be able to understand it, and yet where in the world will you find a reader who never skips a line? Am I not myself the greatest skipper of lines and pages? <br />"I don't deny those symphonies their perfection," continued Paul. "I only deny the importance of that perfection. Those super-sublime symphonies are nothing but cathedrals of the useless. They are inaccessible to man. They are inhuman. We exaggerated their significance. They made us feel inferior. Europe reduced Europe to fifty works of genius that it never understood. Just think of this outrageous inequality: millions of Europeans signifying nothing, against fifty names signifying everything! Class inequality is but an insignificant shortcoming compared to this insulting metaphysical inequality, which turns some into grains of sand while endowing others with the meaning of being!"</i>(294)</blockquote>A couple of more final passages to throw at you concern the "eternal trial" we the living subject dead authors/artists to-- perhaps their only real immortality, a part of Kundera wonders:<blockquote><i>"I'm not talking about hatred for Hemingway! I'm talking about his work. I'm talking about their work! It was necessary to say out loud at last that reading about Hemingway is a thousand times more amusing and instructive than reading Hemingway. It was necessary to show that Hemingway's work is but a coded form of Hemingway's life and that this life was just as poor and meaningless as all our lives. It was necessary to cut Mahler's symphony into little pieces and use it as background music for toilet-paper ads. It was necessary at last to end the terror of the immortals. To overthrow the arrogant power of the Ninth Symphonies and the Fausts!"</i>(295)</blockquote>Pair that with this exchange between Hemingway and Goethe as they meet in the land of the Immortals:<blockquote><i>"What do you want? You were condemned to immortality for the sin of writing books. You explained it to me yourself." <br />Goethe shrugged and said with some pride, "Perhaps our books arc immortal, in a certain sense. Perhaps." He paused and then added softly, with great emphasis, "But we aren't."<br />"Quite the contrary," Hemingway protested bitterly. "Our books will probably soon stop being read. All that will remain of your Faust will be that idiotic opera by Gounod. And maybe also that line about the eternal feminine pulling us somewhere or other..." <br />"Das Ewt0weibliche zieht uns hinan," recited Goethe. <br />"Right. But people will never stop prying into your life, down to the smallest details."</i>(213)</blockquote>Certainly the notion of works of fiction taking the back seat, going forward, to the biography of their authors is a depressing one, and while I do hope that that's not true, I still wonder….<br /><br />I am also left wondering about other things that there is not time for any of us to go into here, such as the influence on this novel of Lacan's ideas concerning the <i>gesture</i>, on Kundera's obvious privileging of Goethe over Rilke and Eluard--and so much more. Fodder not for just another re-read, but a proper study of this remarkable book, one in which I would be wise to strive to be that reader who does not skip over even a single sentence… <br /><br />(To be continued)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11263655560280034294" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_268123490" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/268123490" />
    <a title="Antonomasia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia"><img alt="Antonomasia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605185148p2/6249204.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/268123490?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antonomasia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antonomasia" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia">Antonomasia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=decade-1990s">decade-1990s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=iffp">iffp</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=central-eastern-europe">central-eastern-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=divine-comedy-booklovers">divine-comedy-booklovers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=francophonie">francophonie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=czech">czech</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer268123490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6403888715537748981">Read in two halves, two months apart. In February I was quite intensely irritated, and marked the book two stars... You know those reviews of <i>Nausea</i> and <i>L'Étranger</i>, in which people say the likes of "I used to think this way when I was younger, but it's a bit shit"? (c.f. Hanif Kureishi: "The cruellest thing you can do to Kerouac is re-read him at 38.") I still appreciate those books, but this one looked like my equivalent. I wasn't a teenage existentialist but I was a teenage narcissist, and Kun</span>
  <span id="freeText6403888715537748981" style="display:none">Read in two halves, two months apart. In February I was quite intensely irritated, and marked the book two stars... You know those reviews of <i>Nausea</i> and <i>L'Étranger</i>, in which people say the likes of "I used to think this way when I was younger, but it's a bit shit"? (c.f. Hanif Kureishi: "The cruellest thing you can do to Kerouac is re-read him at 38.") I still appreciate those books, but this one looked like my equivalent. I wasn't a teenage existentialist but I was a teenage narcissist, and Kundera's <i>Immortality</i> seemed to be preaching as wisdom a whole bunch of thoughts which I've gone to considerable and repeated effort to talk myself out of. I suspected myself of being too dismissive and normative towards the book, (also Caliban, mirrors, etc, and yeah this post is a bit mememe) but I simply didn't like the way it made me feel, and what it reminded me of. With its narrative concentrating on the self-designed outward actions of characters as if describing scenes from a film (or teenage / twentysomething self-narration). Mostly surfaces, dull ones at that, interspersed with a few self-consciously Interesting Thoughts, the kind some people would put on an online profile. And also C19th hangers-on of the famous who did what they did primarily because they wanted to be remembered. A lot of it seemed relevant to the social media era - paradoxically: I had this book because it was recommended years ago by someone who's very reclusive and never uses social networks. <br /><br />However. In the third quarter of the novel Kundera critiques the narcissercising, and displays a lot of empathy with the underlying motivations of the characters doing it. So the book became somewhat different from my first assumption.<br /><br />Overall it was a metafictional / anti-novel patchwork thing that was only patchily engaging. Authorial presence interweaved with the characters and their story, whilst doing lots of fine dining, like 1980s Martin Amis turned up to 11. (<i>Immortality</i> was published in 1991, so yeah...) The story of a Parisian professional couple, chilly Agnes and laid-back Paul, and Agnes' melodramatic sister Laura - classic French-film triangle - is punctuated by philosophical reflections on human nature, social behaviour &amp;c, accounts of the Kundera-character's eccentric mates, and the history of Goethe-groupie and eventual woman-of-letters in her own right Bettina von Arnim. The most pointless bit was a 70-odd page survey of the pulling career of a mid-twentieth-century art critic named Rubens; I liked and recognised occasional musings on how people inwardly mark the passing of time, but most of it was nothing I hadn't seen before in a couple of dozen litfic works. Anyway, just about everyone in this sprawl-on-a-theme turns out to be connected by butterflies' wings and hurricanes.<br /><br />I remain slightly puzzled by the quote on the front cover from Nicholas Lezard: "It will make you cleverer, maybe even a better lover. Not many novels can do that." (It was good to learn about Bettina von Arnim and her circle, but the rest was a mix of meh and confirmation of things I'd heard before.) I'd be a little embarrassed by the assumption I agreed with everything I posted three years ago... who knows what someone thinks of stuff they wrote over two decades past, and of it still being used to sell books?<br /><br />Glad to put this book away now - almost every time I saw the title, The Fall song would appear in my head, and I was getting tired of it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6403888715537748981" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer3415886519641029785">I recognize that many may not consider this book on par with The Book of Laughter and Forgetting or The Unbearable Lightness of Being, but throughout this book Kundera confidently displays his mastery of the novel – which he has defined as ‘a poetic meditation on existence.’<br/><br/>The book deals with the way media manipulates popular culture and how technocracy distorts our perception of reality. In addition, there are imagined dialogues between Johann Wolfgang von Goethe and Ernest Hemingway, and Kund</span>
  <span id="freeText3415886519641029785" style="display:none">I recognize that many may not consider this book on par with The Book of Laughter and Forgetting or The Unbearable Lightness of Being, but throughout this book Kundera confidently displays his mastery of the novel – which he has defined as ‘a poetic meditation on existence.’<br /><br />The book deals with the way media manipulates popular culture and how technocracy distorts our perception of reality. In addition, there are imagined dialogues between Johann Wolfgang von Goethe and Ernest Hemingway, and Kundera appears for philosophical discussions with an imagined professor. I have read a couple biographies on Goethe, and it is very clear that Kundera has done much research.<br /><br />Through his characters, Kundera reflects on modem life, exploring the cult of sentimentality, the difference between the individual self and their public image, the conflict between reality and appearance, the difference between love and sexual desire, and the longing for immortality.<br /><br />And, his writing is sprinkled with gems throughout. <br /><br />Kundera, although preferring to lead an anonymous life, has told us told us through interviews that in his novels he has sought to explore what is the nature of existence. He added more rich material to this discussion again.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3415886519641029785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText8172242352390850608" style="display:none">When I first began this book, I thought of it as "enchantingly odd." It's not a light read by any means. But I quickly grew mesmerized by the book, in part because Kundera weaves together a brilliant plot while also offering his philosophies on the characters' actions and behaviors. So instead of merely describing what people do (which would be interesting enough), he beautifully describes the enduring meaning and themes of these behaviors. For example, in one part, a character decides he no longer wants to see a woman with whom he had a one-night stand...Kundera writes, "Her shoes would walk the world with a tad more melancholy than before." The book is full of these little gems. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6711018428824423482">Another book I'd give 4.5 stars to if I could. Kundera writes like a philosopher most of the time, but when he is really good, he writes (prose) like a poet. The line that really jumped at me, and captures the essence of this book in my opinion, is "The emotion of love gives all of us a misleading illusion of knowing the other." A bit cynical perhaps but understandable given the context of Kundera's life and writing, and not necessarily untrue. Like many of other Kundera's books, it is full of c</span>
  <span id="freeText6711018428824423482" style="display:none">Another book I'd give 4.5 stars to if I could. Kundera writes like a philosopher most of the time, but when he is really good, he writes (prose) like a poet. The line that really jumped at me, and captures the essence of this book in my opinion, is "The emotion of love gives all of us a misleading illusion of knowing the other." A bit cynical perhaps but understandable given the context of Kundera's life and writing, and not necessarily untrue. Like many of other Kundera's books, it is full of characters who are on a journey of self-discovery, and also like his other works, Kundera's ingenuity and intellect (or is it intellectualness?) comes across. Unfortunately, I think this is at the expense of emotions and feelings of his characters, which I feel Kundera did not really treat or develop adequately (hence not 5 stars).</span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3491184-julie-rylie?shelf=strongly-recommend">strongly-recommend</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3491184-julie-rylie?shelf=changed-me">changed-me</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3491184-julie-rylie?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156139744" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10466695628506246221">It always makes me frightened to write any review on Kundera... I love him so much it hurts, and i love him enough not to be able to express what it fells like to read any of his books because they leave me speechless and with a sense of accomplishment and a strong feeling i inhaled every single word. <br/><br/>In the beginning he states this really interesting speech about gestures, that won me over at the first chapter. And he even talked about what i always inquire: "the russian sentiment". He calls it</span>
  <span id="freeText10466695628506246221" style="display:none">It always makes me frightened to write any review on Kundera... I love him so much it hurts, and i love him enough not to be able to express what it fells like to read any of his books because they leave me speechless and with a sense of accomplishment and a strong feeling i inhaled every single word. <br /><br />In the beginning he states this really interesting speech about gestures, that won me over at the first chapter. And he even talked about what i always inquire: "the russian sentiment". He calls it "the Slavic soul". Now it leaves me no room for deciding who is my favorite author - ever - . <br /><br />And i thought i was being original with this russian sentiment thing, i always wanted to do an essay about it. Since i started reading russian/ ukranian authors i had the feeling they had another way of showing and understanding emotion, and i mean, in a really different way compared to the rest of the (at least literary) world. And Kundera knows it and he felt the same way i did, which is scary. It's scary when a person writes and you think it's for you. It's scary when a person can define so well the conceptions of emotions, the exact same way you think of them. i fell almost delusional at times. <br /><br />And i cannot forget about this sentence, this brilliant sentence "'I think therefore I am' is the statement of an intellectual who underrates toothache", lovely, lovely, lovely (!)<br /><br />Oh, and another very very important thing... An author that makes himself the narrator and includes himself in his own book and still manages not to make you fell nauseated or think that he is just a filthy megalomaniac, gotta be something out of the ordinary. This was quite special for me, especially after I read "Veronica decides to die" by Paulo Coelho, which was one the most horrid books ever. Kundera can do it and you can even get the feeling that you <i>need</i> him in the book, he is there for a reason. <br /><br />Oh, Kundera, Kundera, just bought 2 more of your books last week &lt;3 </span>
  <a data-text-id="10466695628506246221" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Lorraine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/229794-lorraine"><img alt="Lorraine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1186165454p2/229794.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/163005287?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lorraine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lorraine" href="/user/show/229794-lorraine">Lorraine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>
